        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer326273105" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer328771539010549441"><a href='http://www.theaceofclouds.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>Christmas 2010: I realised that I had got stuck in a rut. I was re-reading old favourites again and again, waiting for a few trusted authors to release new works. Something had to be done.<br/><br/>On the spur of the moment I set myself a challenge, to read every book to have won the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2' rel='nofollow noopener'>Locus Sci-Fi</a> award. That’s 35 books, 6 of which I’d previously read, leaving 29 titles by 14 authors who were new to me.<br/><br/>While working through this reading list I got married, went on my honeymoon, switched career and beca</b></span>
  <span id="freeText328771539010549441" style="display:none"><a href="http://www.theaceofclouds.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Christmas 2010: I realised that I had got stuck in a rut. I was re-reading old favourites again and again, waiting for a few trusted authors to release new works. Something had to be done.<br><br>On the spur of the moment I set myself a challenge, to read every book to have won the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2" rel="nofollow noopener">Locus Sci-Fi</a> award. That’s 35 books, 6 of which I’d previously read, leaving 29 titles by 14 authors who were new to me.<br><br>While working through this reading list I got married, went on my honeymoon, switched career and became a father. As such these stories became imprinted on my memory as the soundtrack to the happiest period in my life (so far).</b><br><br><i>* Published as two books, </i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10771496-blackout" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a><i> and </i><b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8733971-all-clear" rel="nofollow noopener">All Clear</a>
</b><i> do not work as two separate stories. This is one tale spread over two volumes. As such I am writing one extra long review and spreading it between the two books. <br><br>** As this is a time-travel book with events reported in a non-linear manner, I am writing this review as a ‘non-linear’ ‘time-travel’ review. I’ve labelled the paragraphs and then jumbled them up between the two reviews. You poor schmucks have to make sense of my drivel (if such a thing is possible) – think of it like a puzzle, or a game, yeah? <br><br>*** Sometimes I wonder; is my brilliance perhaps outshone only by my modesty?</i><br><br>Begin your journey by going through <b>THE BROWN DOOR WITH THE BRASS HANDLE</b><br><br>Parts of your journey will occur on <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/326273083" rel="nofollow noopener">this review of BLACKOUT</a><br><br>### <b>THE OLD SUBWAY</b><br>So let’s have a look at some of the regular complaints…<br><br>1) I read the first book and it just ends without any sort of resolution or even a proper cliff-hanger… it just ends! I’m very unsatisfied.<br><br> - Dear reader, read both books together and judge them as one story. She could have published as one book – it would have been thick enough to stand on when you need to reach on top of the wardrobe – but from a narrative perspective, it would have made sense. Be grateful that she split it into two portable tomes! <br><i>CONTINUE <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/326273083" rel="nofollow noopener">ACROSS THE QUAD</a></i><br><br>### <b>FORM WTF/83/CC</b><br>Overall, I’d give it 3.5 stars, and I thought the second half was slightly stronger, hence the 3|4 split. It didn’t need to be so long. It could have slimmed down to a thousand pages in one novel and not lost a huge amount of its impact – that wasn’t a massive issue for me, but would have pulled it up that half-star to a 4. A more quirky protagonist would have given it a little extra spark – Polly, Mike and Eileen are all lovely people, but they’re all fairly middle-of-the-road types – the most memorable characters are the Hodbin children. A bit of a curveball on one of the leads would have pumped it up to 4.5 for me. Finally, a better explanation of Dunworthy’s behaviour – switching all the assignments around (but letting them go ahead, unprepared) seemed negligent and inept. I know the incompetence of bureaucracy is one of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Willis</a>’ regular themes – but here I felt it wasn’t supported or justified adequately. Correcting this would have seen me 5-star the story with a giddy grin on my funny little face.<br><i>CONTINUE BY TAKING YOUR FORM TO <u>COUNTER 6F</u></i><br><br>### <b>THE BROWN DOOR WITH THE BRASS HANDLE</b><br>I know they get a weaker version (made by a different manufacturer) in New Zealand, and the Aussies have an equivalent called Vegemite – but can you buy Marmite in the States? If you’ve never heard of Marmite, it’s a thick, black, salty syrup – a kind of gloopy yeast extract – which is eaten spread on toast. It’s got a strong and distinctive flavour. I don’t think <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Connie Willis</a> mentions Marmite at any point in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10771496-blackout" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a>
</i> or <b>
  <i>
    <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8733971-all-clear" rel="nofollow noopener">All Clear</a>
  </i>
</b>, but the whole production reeks of Marmite to me. For as long as I can remember, Marmite’s advertising slogan has been “love it or hate it”. If you offer a stranger some Marmite on toast, either their eyes light up in delight or their face screws up in disgust. There’s no middle ground with Marmite – it’s a polarising product – and in the UK anything which produces an equivalent, strongly polarised response can be branded as eliciting ‘a Marmite response’. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Connie Willis</a> is something of a Marmite author, and with this duology she’s reached a new peak of Marmiteyness. Vintage Marmite – aged for an even stronger reaction.<br><i>CONTINUE DOWN <u>THE LONG CORRIDOR</u></i><br><br>### <b>DUTY OFFICE</b><br>It’s not the first time I’ve noticed this sort of response to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Willis</a>’ work. Her ’93 novel, (and fellow Locus/Hugo winner, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book" rel="nofollow noopener">Doomsday Book</a>
</i>, prompted similar wails of praise as well as angry gnashings of teeth. I find this curious. So what is up with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Willis</a>?<br><i>CONTINUE BY TAKING <u>THE OLD LIFT</u></i><br><br>### <b>THE LONG CORRIDOR</b><br>How are these for some polarised review quotes…<br><br>The lovers:<br><blockquote> “This is a very impressive novel, and obviously a labor of love for Willis. I expect one of these books will win her another Hugo.” – <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/127254535" rel="nofollow noopener">Jamie</a>
</blockquote><blockquote>“This is a beautifully crafted book,” “It’s ingenious and a great deal of fun. It’s funny, tragic, romantic, heartwarming, and completely engrossing.” – <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/134765157" rel="nofollow noopener">Lisa Vegan</a>
</blockquote><blockquote>“Willis manages something transcendent,” “these volumes are the greatest work of science fiction ever written.” – <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89021316" rel="nofollow noopener">Kaethe</a>
</blockquote><i>CONTINUE UP <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/326273083" rel="nofollow noopener">THE NARROW STAIRS</a></i><br><br>### <b>THE OLD LIFT</b><br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10771496-blackout" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a>
</i>/<i>
  <b>
    <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8733971-all-clear" rel="nofollow noopener">All Clear</a>
  </b>
</i> is part of the Oxford Time Travel series. Future Oxford historians go back in time to live in WW2 and study various aspects of life at that time. Something goes wrong with their ‘drops’, the portals they use to travel through time, and they are stranded in the past and must survive in wartime Britain. It’s a solid premise that doesn’t vary too far from the successful template of <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book" rel="nofollow noopener">Doomsday Book</a>
</i> (where a single historian is stranded in the middle ages).<br><i>CONTINUE BY TAKING <u>THE OLD SUBWAY</u></i><br><br>### <b>COUNTER 6F</b><br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10771496-blackout" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a>
</i> and <i>
  <b>
    <a href="" rel="nofollow noopener">All Clear</a>
  </b>
</i> (together) won the Locus Sci-Fi and Hugo awards in 2011, beating off competition from: <br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16094.Lois_McMaster_Bujold" rel="nofollow noopener">Bujold</a>’s latest <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/40380-vorkosigan-saga" rel="nofollow noopener">Vorkosigan Saga</a> - <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7841670-cryoburn" rel="nofollow noopener">Cryoburn</a>
</i>, <br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5807106.Iain_M_Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Bank</a>’s latest <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/49118-culture" rel="nofollow noopener">Culture</a> novel – <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7937744-surface-detail" rel="nofollow noopener">Surface Detail</a>
</i><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3153776.Mira_Grant" rel="nofollow noopener">Mira Grant</a>’s debut, the GR Sci-Fi Readers Choice winner – <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7094569-feed" rel="nofollow noopener">Feed</a>
</i><br>… and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25376.Ian_McDonald" rel="nofollow noopener">Ian McDonald</a> – <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6993091-the-dervish-house" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dervish House</a>
</i> (which sounds awesome!)<br><i>CONTINUE BY TAKING YOUR FORM TO <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/326273083" rel="nofollow noopener">THE PIDGEONHOLES</a></i><br><br><b>After this I read: </b><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/329888777" rel="nofollow noopener">A Madness of Angels</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="328771539010549441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_125285330" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125285330" />
    <a title="Elizabeth Hunter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/915317-elizabeth-hunter"><img alt="Elizabeth Hunter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203392574p2/915317.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125285330?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth Hunter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth Hunter" href="/user/show/915317-elizabeth-hunter">Elizabeth Hunter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/915317-elizabeth-hunter?shelf=sf">sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125285330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer323746411238516599">Three stars is a compromise rating here. I have loved much of Connie Willis' work and her strengths keep growing. There is much to love in this two-book story: fantastic period detail, including real consideration for both how the period looks from a remove and how it was experienced by its "contemps"; nicely drawn characters who react in plausible ways to the situations they face; intricate plotting and an interesting story to tell. Unfortunately, there is much here to make one want to pull a D</span>
  <span id="freeText323746411238516599" style="display:none">Three stars is a compromise rating here. I have loved much of Connie Willis' work and her strengths keep growing. There is much to love in this two-book story: fantastic period detail, including real consideration for both how the period looks from a remove and how it was experienced by its "contemps"; nicely drawn characters who react in plausible ways to the situations they face; intricate plotting and an interesting story to tell. Unfortunately, there is much here to make one want to pull a Dorothy Parker and hurl the book across the room with great force. The endless fretting--while perfectly plausible--was incredibly tedious, as was the detailed explanation of every single trip anyone in the book took, whether across all of England or one neighborhood in London. Being very familiar with London myself, I appreciated the accuracy of Willis' research, but writing is not the place where you get credit for showing all your work. Finally, given the religious personnel and iconography evoked, it seemed disingenous that no one ever suggested--even as a theory to be rejected--that one explanation for the manipulations of the "time continuum" that they might be talking about God. Religious explanations aren't my cup of tea and might not be appropriate to a science fiction story, but under the circumstances, the omission felt deliberate and coy. <br /><br />Overall, had it not been for a friend wanting my thoughts on the ending, I might not have read the second book. I would have missed a lot of good--any time anything was actually happening, it's a great read--but spared myself a lot of tedious page-turning. </span>
  <a data-text-id="323746411238516599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_180769732" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180769732" />
    <a title="Dhitri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4157851-dhitri"><img alt="Dhitri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603603086p2/4157851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/180769732?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dhitri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dhitri" href="/user/show/4157851-dhitri">Dhitri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780345522696" href="/book/show/9536163-all-clear">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer180769732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4767774944461794512">Oh I really don't know where to start: This book has been a disappointment and an utter waste of time. I dragged myself through 'Blackout' and persisted with this one, thinking that it will be all worth it. But despite of the twist in the end and what many have termed 'brilliant' finale, I still can't get over the fact that the omniscient narrative of Ms Willis, in which she follows each miniscule thought of the time travellers, really time consuming, boring, to a point it became truly annoying </span>
  <span id="freeText4767774944461794512" style="display:none">Oh I really don't know where to start: This book has been a disappointment and an utter waste of time. I dragged myself through 'Blackout' and persisted with this one, thinking that it will be all worth it. But despite of the twist in the end and what many have termed 'brilliant' finale, I still can't get over the fact that the omniscient narrative of Ms Willis, in which she follows each miniscule thought of the time travellers, really time consuming, boring, to a point it became truly annoying and in the end just runed everything for me. Yes, I do find her take on time travelling intriguing (sending historians back in time to study history without affecting history) and some of characters have the potential to actually be memorable, but whatever empathy I had for them was just offset by their constant complaining, self-doubting, arrogance and two sentences; "Where is the retrieval team?" and "This is time travel". Yes we got that! Insert big sigh here. Did she really have to repeat that over and over again over 700+ pages? In short: Not worth your time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4767774944461794512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_131032962" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/131032962" />
    <a title="Sherwood Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/162084-sherwood-smith"><img alt="Sherwood Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543007644p2/162084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/131032962?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sherwood Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sherwood Smith" href="/user/show/162084-sherwood-smith">Sherwood Smith</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162084-sherwood-smith?shelf=alt-history">alt-history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer131032962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16975699092233981134">Aside from horror (which I avoid) I really, really dislike comedy that depends on humiliation of the helpless, and anxious, frenetic running around with nothing getting done.<br/><br/>These two books were nothing but running anxiously around, the entire thousand plus pages. Because of that, I couldn't read straight through--I had to put the book down after a chapter or two, but Willis's writing is so good, her scenes so vivid, and above all, the numinous moments so lovely (and other moments so poignant) t</span>
  <span id="freeText16975699092233981134" style="display:none">Aside from horror (which I avoid) I really, really dislike comedy that depends on humiliation of the helpless, and anxious, frenetic running around with nothing getting done.<br /><br />These two books were nothing but running anxiously around, the entire thousand plus pages. Because of that, I couldn't read straight through--I had to put the book down after a chapter or two, but Willis's writing is so good, her scenes so vivid, and above all, the numinous moments so lovely (and other moments so poignant) that I persisted. It takes a lot of draw to get me to read any of these three things. She carried it off; the end is beautifully done.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16975699092233981134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2982566246" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2982566246" />
    <a title="Jemppu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/85472654-jemppu"><img alt="Jemppu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574378182p2/85472654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2982566246?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jemppu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jemppu" href="/user/show/85472654-jemppu">Jemppu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/20326697-all-clear">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2982566246" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14448047799552993471">Perhaps this wasn't the best of book(s) to have started with Willis. As much as I wanted to like it, the story just didn't rise to the occasion, but dragged on and on and on from one book to another with hardly any suspense or change in it's tiringly frenzied pace. I ultimately had to accept, that despite it's few scattered moments, the destination hadn't enough of an impact to justify the taxing journey.<br/><br/>The narration was meandering and repetitive, and the approach got lost somewhere in executio</span>
  <span id="freeText14448047799552993471" style="display:none">Perhaps this wasn't the best of book(s) to have started with Willis. As much as I wanted to like it, the story just didn't rise to the occasion, but dragged on and on and on from one book to another with hardly any suspense or change in it's tiringly frenzied pace. I ultimately had to accept, that despite it's few scattered moments, the destination hadn't enough of an impact to justify the taxing journey.<br /><br />The narration was meandering and repetitive, and the approach got lost somewhere in execution: the future outsider's POV with it's inability to commit to a moment due to the constant expectation of getting out, didn't feel it really allowed for a portrayal of the life or the war experience of the era in a fully convincing or immersive manner, yet neither did the approach of 'a lone traveler trapped in the past with nothing to do but wait for rescue and speculate' lend itself to a compelling time-travel narrative, when the reality of their own time never really got established or served in contrast <i>(or when the most ardent thing they had to say about their 'captive' era seemed to be "air raids, misbehaving kids and hosiery runs are all quite inconvenient")</i>.<br /><br />The premise appears so ripe for a more epic time adventure, yet Willis doesn't seem to have the want to explore ie. the possible cultural/societal differences between the eras to add depth to the characters' unusual misplacement situation, instead, seems rather content to just run around in a period setting, playing with situational comedy and shallow staged drama, with the excuse of not needing to commit to a full on period work either.<br /><br />The most genuine experience the reading of the two books thus provided felt like that of a tourist group separated and lost in a foreign city, without money, IDs or phones, fretting over transportation, place names, accommodations, crucial travel dates, and just frantically rushing about confused trying to get by and re-group: the comprehensive, irksome experience of a vacation gone awry.<br /><br />It also felt regrettably wasteful of the amount of factual knowledge to spent it on a creatively/dramatically/structurally mediocre work.<br /><br />I realize this might not be the best (edited) of Willis' work, and so shall still remain curious to find out as to why they are as beloved as they are.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14448047799552993471" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3291798192" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3291798192" />
    <a title="lucky little cat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38160628-lucky-little-cat"><img alt="lucky little cat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600336662p2/38160628.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3291798192?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="lucky little cat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="lucky little cat" href="/user/show/38160628-lucky-little-cat">lucky little cat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=4-anglophiles">4-anglophiles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=and-not-in-the-good-way">and-not-in-the-good-way</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=borrow-don-t-buy">borrow-don-t-buy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=disappointing">disappointing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=historiche">historiche</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=space-opera-time-travel">space-opera-time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=ww2">ww2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=murtaugh-list">murtaugh-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38160628-lucky-little-cat?shelf=not-a-trace-of-irony">not-a-trace-of-irony</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3291798192" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1291590476688579514"><b>Awfulness this persistent requires a full-sized keyboard and all ten fingers to describe.*</b><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1587622914ra/29340534.gif' width='450/' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <i>Cutting my losses, DNF at 90%. I can see the future, and it's shamelessly predictable.</i><br/><br/>BOTH volumes in this series <b>should have been trimmed drastically.</b> Instead, readers are dragged through several hundred extraneous pages of the time-traveling main characters' circular agonizing over whether they've irreparably screwed up the historical timeline. Have they doomed England and themselves? Maybe. Maybe not. M</span>
  <span id="freeText1291590476688579514" style="display:none"><b>Awfulness this persistent requires a full-sized keyboard and all ten fingers to describe.*</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1587622914ra/29340534.gif" width="450/" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <i>Cutting my losses, DNF at 90%. I can see the future, and it's shamelessly predictable.</i><br><br>BOTH volumes in this series <b>should have been trimmed drastically.</b> Instead, readers are dragged through several hundred extraneous pages of the time-traveling main characters' circular agonizing over whether they've irreparably screwed up the historical timeline. Have they doomed England and themselves? Maybe. Maybe not. Maybe. Maybe not. Repeat repeat repeat.<br><br>The characters' neuroses are rooted squarely in the author’s misplaced humility. Willis is a reverent historian. <b>She is clearly aware that by assigning time-traveling heroes to “help” save the Allies in World War II, she risks undercutting (and cheapening) the true life stories of those who experienced the war.</b> The author's response to this dilemma is to make her characters brood and soul-search endlessly, presumably so they'll seem less like upstarts. Rest easy, readers, the time-travelers are Taking Their Responsibilities Seriously. And invoking the apropos <i>Hamlet</i> quotes while stalling! These <i>numerous</i> woe-is-me, do-I-dare paragraphs are empty, annoying, and distracting. And <b>they bulk up the page count unforgivably.</b><br><br><b>Nothing in the rest of the story adequately compensates readers for all of those deadweight pages.</b> If you’ve read any decent World War II historical fiction, <i>Blackout</i> has almost nothing new to show you. Worse, you’ll visit the familiar historical scenes in the company of two plucky time-traveling young heroines with nearly interchangeable personalities and a male counterpart who’s equal parts cardboard and Goodness. <br><br><b>Supporting characters will try your patience even more.</b> We get the full church-basement’s complement: a shrewish landlady, louche-but-good-hearted shopgirls, knitting spinsters, husband-hunting WACs, harmless balding men, and a silvery Olivier-esque Master Thespian who spouts Shakespeare lines instead of <i>ever</i> just <i>talking.</i> These are stock characters who behave <i>exactly</i> as you would expect, and the effect on the reader after several hundred pages is soul-shriveling. <b>Add to the cast Alf and Binnie Hodbin, aDORably scruffy orphan delinquents in the mode of the Herdmans of <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/239810.The_Best_Christmas_Pageant_Ever__The_Herdmans__1_" title="The Best Christmas Pageant Ever (The Herdmans #1) by Barbara Robinson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Best Christmas Pageant Ever</a>.</i> Willis all-too-frequently relies on the Hodbins’ major talents (squabbling, squalling and stealing)</b> for comic relief, with predictable results.<br><br>And all of these characters come back for more in volume 2, <i>All Clear.</i> (Although the Master Thespian does get actual conversational speeches this go-round, and the Hodbins re-enter relatively late in the action.) The inevitable wrap-up begins in the last 20% of the pages, and features a character death so mercilessly telegraphed that the extra manipulative pathos stings like an insult. (“Dead?” Blink-blink. “_____’s <i>staying</i> dead this time?”) <b>Altogether, it’s like spending 26-plus hours with a mediocre old-time radio melodrama, complete with <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NyACiWf9gnU" rel="nofollow noopener">AM on-air whine.</a></b><br><br><b>keywords:</b> just put <i>all</i> the parts of <i>all</i> the rough drafts in there, the fans won't mind; I can out-martyr all of you just by staying put; how I miss my Air Raid Adelaide costume; so <i>that's</i> why it's called a Holman Bible; well fudge, there goes our pantomime; bright green coats fill me with dread, too<br><br> <b>Pet Peeves:</b><br><br>WWII homefront folks who talked this <i>incessantly</i> and accurately about where the bombs were going to fall would have been locked up pronto as conspirators.<br><br>I’m especially irked and disappointed that Willis drags Dunworthy (her Dumbledore-esque stalwart of <i>The Doomsday Book</i> and <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i>) into this mess as a helplessly frail old man. There's nothing like crapping all over your own legend.<br><br>*I'm reviewing both volumes of the series together here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1291590476688579514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/138403904" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/202724-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601676054p2/202724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553592887" href="/book/show/10617408-all-clear">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=award-winners">award-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=all-timefavorites">all-timefavorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer138403904" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7636871943881971904">Breathtaking. And, let's face it, part of the reason why I was crying at the end wasn't just because the ending was so perfect, but because I know that I will never write a book as amazing as this. <br/><br/>Not only has Willis crafted an intricately layered time travel novel, but it's also an outstanding piece of historical fiction. She has an encyclopedic knowledge of WWII, and makes you feel the horror and hardship of life in England during the war in a way that no other author can. (At least none in m</span>
  <span id="freeText7636871943881971904" style="display:none">Breathtaking. And, let's face it, part of the reason why I was crying at the end wasn't just because the ending was so perfect, but because I know that I will never write a book as amazing as this. <br /><br />Not only has Willis crafted an intricately layered time travel novel, but it's also an outstanding piece of historical fiction. She has an encyclopedic knowledge of WWII, and makes you feel the horror and hardship of life in England during the war in a way that no other author can. (At least none in my experience.) Her characters, both the time travelers and the "contemps" are real and completely engaging. Even when a random shopgirl or crabby landlady gets killed in the Blitz, you feel their loss. And how Willis kept track of each of the characters, some of them with multiple aliases and existing in multiple threads, I will never know. <br /><br />There were surprises and twists, humor, romance, adventure, mystery, history, and science all wrapped together into one fabulous package. This review, by the way, also applies to the first half of the story, BLACKOUT, which you must read first. And you must read them, yes, you must.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7636871943881971904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1186736868" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12725155938557610868">Walk-on appearances by Alan Turing, Agatha Christie, Queen Elizabeth, General Patton ... we didn't quite get to have afternoon tea with the King and Winston Churchill, but almost. Puh-leeze!<br/><br/>Added to the need to cram in every bit of information possible on the Blitz and the build-up to D-Day, Connie Willis gave us in this book and "Blackout" two horrendous Cockney brats who turned out to be a crucial part of the plot. I suspect the problem is that Willis' books are totally plot driven and her cha</span>
  <span id="freeText12725155938557610868" style="display:none">Walk-on appearances by Alan Turing, Agatha Christie, Queen Elizabeth, General Patton ... we didn't quite get to have afternoon tea with the King and Winston Churchill, but almost. Puh-leeze!<br /><br />Added to the need to cram in every bit of information possible on the Blitz and the build-up to D-Day, Connie Willis gave us in this book and "Blackout" two horrendous Cockney brats who turned out to be a crucial part of the plot. I suspect the problem is that Willis' books are totally plot driven and her characters are very two-dimensional. So the irritating people who cause problems in each of her books seem to be totally appalling and without any redeeming features.<br /><br />In the department of bad editing (or a lack of it) I was very surprised to find that one of the characters had a "tuppenny piece" which she was trying to pass as legal tender when purchasing a postcard. It was news to me that such a coin existed in 1940!<br /><br />Disappointing because I enjoyed a book of her short stories, and also "Doomsday Book", but enough is enough! </span>
  <a data-text-id="12725155938557610868" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3016456755" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3016456755" />
    <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud"><img alt="Oleksandr Zholud" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506182069p2/4743119.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3016456755?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oleksandr Zholud" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud">Oleksandr Zholud</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3016456755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11831463090747893398">This is the second volume of the two-book series, so read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307.Blackout__All_Clear___1_' title='Blackout (All Clear, #1) by Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Blackout</a> before reading this review.<br/><br/>Our three time travelers found each other and now together try to get out from the 1940 blitz in London. Once <br/>again it was a mix of great historical narrative with a great care about details and that 'close call' soup opera-ness that irks me: why, oh why another traveler was just behind the wall/next door/was coughing and unable to talk?! And if talking than starting from far away, so they never reach t</span>
  <span id="freeText11831463090747893398" style="display:none">This is the second volume of the two-book series, so read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307.Blackout__All_Clear___1_" title="Blackout (All Clear, #1) by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a> before reading this review.<br /><br />Our three time travelers found each other and now together try to get out from the 1940 blitz in London. Once <br />again it was a mix of great historical narrative with a great care about details and that 'close call' soup opera-ness that irks me: why, oh why another traveler was just behind the wall/next door/was coughing and unable to talk?! And if talking than starting from far away, so they never reach the essential info! It is fine once or twice, but when it continues page after page… The protagonists have done time travel for years and studied others, who did so, but they are <u>always</u> surprised and confused that person A can meet person B in the latter’s future but the former’s past. It’s time travel 101!<br /><br />One of the silent characters in the book is a famous mystery writer <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie" title="Agatha Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">Agatha Christie</a> and the knot of intertwined time travels can be seen as a homage to her. I know a lot of people who adores her mysteries, but I’m not one of them. As a book-devouring teen I read a score of her mysteries and quite soon I was able to predict, who the killer will be even before the murder - her clues stuck out like sore thumbs for me (except for special cases like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/853510.Murder_on_the_Orient_Express__Hercule_Poirot___10_" title="Murder on the Orient Express (Hercule Poirot, #10) by Agatha Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">Murder on the Orient Express</a>) and here the clues have been obvious for me as well.<br /><br />What I disliked is that constant assumption that the war can be either won or lost instead of continuum of possible victories with different schedules and death tolls. And there can be nothing bad after the war, like an attack from the USSR (see real life Operation Unthinkable ordered by British prime minister Winston Churchill in 1945 at the end of World War II in Europe). Or cold war turning nuclear in the 60s or 70s. or infinity (!) of other disasters, which could have happened after the VE Day.<br /><br />Mopreover, if one looks at the economics of the Third Reich it was doomed against the US - the great book on Nazi economics is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/711592.The_Wages_of_Destruction_The_Making_and_Breaking_of_the_Nazi_Economy" title="The Wages of Destruction The Making and Breaking of the Nazi Economy by Adam Tooze" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wages of Destruction: The Making and Breaking of the Nazi Economy</a>. Even if we assume that Hitler won over the Britain and USSR (both questionable even with losing Dunkirk) and got A-bomb by 1945 (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16884.The_Making_of_the_Atomic_Bomb" title="The Making of the Atomic Bomb by Richard Rhodes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Making of the Atomic Bomb</a> says that in 1945 Germans were at several years behind and with no capacities to keep the project) he still had no chances I think.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11831463090747893398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2425816044" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2425816044" />
    <a title="Vanessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/752176-vanessa"><img alt="Vanessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199757927p2/752176.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2425816044?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa" href="/user/show/752176-vanessa">Vanessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/752176-vanessa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2425816044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10382973384114526177"><i>To do something for someone or something you loved--England or Shakespeare or a dog or the Hodbins or history--wasn't a sacrifice at all. Even if it cost you your freedom, your life, your youth.</i><br/> --Connie Willis, <i>All Clear</i><br/><br/><i>And we know we shall win<br/>As we are confident<br/>In the victory<br/>Of good over evil</i><br/><br/> --Bob Marley, "War"<br/><br/>Oh boy. This book. I have a lot to say. Or rather, I have a jumble of thoughts and emotions that I will try to pour out into coherent speech. Bear with me. <br/><br/>First of all, this is part of</span>
  <span id="freeText10382973384114526177" style="display:none"><i>To do something for someone or something you loved--England or Shakespeare or a dog or the Hodbins or history--wasn't a sacrifice at all. Even if it cost you your freedom, your life, your youth.</i><br /> --Connie Willis, <i>All Clear</i><br /><br /><i>And we know we shall win<br />As we are confident<br />In the victory<br />Of good over evil</i><br /><br /> --Bob Marley, "War"<br /><br />Oh boy. This book. I have a lot to say. Or rather, I have a jumble of thoughts and emotions that I will try to pour out into coherent speech. Bear with me. <br /><br />First of all, this is part of Willis' Oxford Travel Series, but these novels are loosely related and you do not have to read them in order. There are a few asides to the events in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book" rel="nofollow noopener"> Doomsday Book</a>, but they are minor and not spoilery (but you should still read that book, because it's one of my all-time favorites.) You DO need to read the book prior to this one, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307-blackout" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a>, and you need to plan to read them fairly close together while your memory is fresh. <br /><br />These books combined are 1200+ pages. Yes, they are long. Yes, they can sometimes be repetitive. Yes, they are full of Willis' favored trope of missed connections and misunderstandings; her novel <i>Passage</i> (which is not part of the Oxford series) had a running joke which was later used to dramatic effect about the Byzantine layout of the hospital where the story takes place. But if this book were shorter, it wouldn't be the same story with the same impact. You feel like you really know these characters by the end and have shared so much in their daily sorrows, deprivations, friendships, and victories. And Willis gives you a real taste of what life during the Blitz was like. The amount of research she had to do for this is staggering: characters discuss on multiple occasions where bombs fell on a particular day, there is a wealth of period detail, there are side journeys into Bletchley Park and Fortitude South, St. Paul's appears so much that it's practically a character and I'm convinced Willis memorized its roof to cellar layout. And bonus, there's a cameo from Agatha Christie. <br /><br />My only real complaint with the story is <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Mike faking his own death so he could better find a way to get Polly home. First, that doesn't make any sense. Second, to put Polly and Eileen through thinking that he was killed during a raid and then LETTING THEM HAVE A MEMORIAL FOR HIM WHEN HE WAS STILL ALIVE wasn't just insensitive, it didn't make sense as something that character would do. Of course, the reader is pretty sure he's still alive because there are a lot of clues that Mike and Ernest are the same person. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />But despite any flaws of repetition (which were for me almost entirely in the first 50 pages to get the reader up to speed on what happened in the previous book), this is still a five star read and earns a berth on my Favorites shelf because it's such a staggering achievement. And by the end I just loved these damn people so much and the way the whole story is brought together is so fulfilling. When near the end one character says of another, "We're going to be such good friends," I openly wept because it was true. <br /><br /><br />Willis's intent is to highlight and celebrate the bravery of ordinary people during WWII: the ARP wardens, the FANY's, the British citizens who kept on living their day-to-day lives with grace and fortitude. And her timely message that I need to remind myself of daily is <b>no act of goodness is ever wasted</b>. The smallest actions matter.<br /><br />And with the world, including our own country, as close to fascism as it has been 70 years, it's imperative to ask what can we do? What can you do?<br /><br />What can I do? <br /><br />It matters. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10382973384114526177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_123807415" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/123807415" />
    <a title="Margaret" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/162759-margaret"><img alt="Margaret" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1234732285p2/162759.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/123807415?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margaret" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margaret" href="/user/show/162759-margaret">Margaret</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=fantasy-and-science-fiction">fantasy-and-science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=wwii">wwii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=authors-vw">authors-vw</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=2010-read">2010-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer123807415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10514178205203601887"><i>All Clear</i> concludes the story Willis began in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307.Blackout__Blackout___1_' title='Blackout (Blackout, #1) by Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Blackout</a>. It's unfortunate that the books had to be published separately, because they really are two halves of the same book and can't really be considered separately.<br/><br/>In Willis's time-travelling-Oxford-historians universe, several historians have been sent back to various points in England during World War II: Polly, in London masquerading as a shopgirl; Eileen, working as a maid in the country with evacuated children; and Michael, studying acts of </span>
  <span id="freeText10514178205203601887" style="display:none"><i>All Clear</i> concludes the story Willis began in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307.Blackout__Blackout___1_" title="Blackout (Blackout, #1) by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a>. It's unfortunate that the books had to be published separately, because they really are two halves of the same book and can't really be considered separately.<br /><br />In Willis's time-travelling-Oxford-historians universe, several historians have been sent back to various points in England during World War II: Polly, in London masquerading as a shopgirl; Eileen, working as a maid in the country with evacuated children; and Michael, studying acts of heroism at Dunkirk. Each historian slowly realizes that something has gone very wrong with the time travel mechanism, and they appear to be trapped in the past, while on the other end, James Dunworthy (head of the time travellers) and Colin Templer (a student in love with Polly) struggle to find the marooned historians.<br /><br />If I had to make a couple of small criticisms, I would say that the books are a little too long and could have been edited down a bit. Also, the relationship between Polly and Colin could have been more developed; it comes to a head with a suddenness that didn't quite convince me. In the end, though, I became so invested in the characters and the detailed setting that I didn't much care how long the books were; I read happily late into the night to finish <i>All Clear</i>.<br /><br />The complex narrative could easily have become confusing, but Willis balances her viewpoints and weaves the story's threads together superbly: everything left open-ended at the end of <i>Blackout</i> is slowly brought together and wrapped up by the end of <i>All Clear</i>. She even brings in bits of other works in this universe (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog" title="To Say Nothing of the Dog by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">To Say Nothing of the Dog</a> and the wonderful short story "Fire Watch"), which makes it even more satisfying. Her evocation of wartime Britain is beautifully detailed and understanding, and I loved her celebration of small heroic acts, easily overlooked, yet vitally important.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10514178205203601887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer153092687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7539814487927017660">I didn't much like the first volume of this novel, but I picked up the second part hoping that -- much like Robin Hobb did in her last book -- Willis would make everything pay off in a satisfying way. Nope. Not this time, at least not for me.<br/><br/>There are some good scenes (an action sequence in the middle, and the conclusion) that kept me from giving this one star, but overall this is overblown and unwieldy. I get that this is a tribute to the ordinary folk who won the Battle of Britain, and as the </span>
  <span id="freeText7539814487927017660" style="display:none">I didn't much like the first volume of this novel, but I picked up the second part hoping that -- much like Robin Hobb did in her last book -- Willis would make everything pay off in a satisfying way. Nope. Not this time, at least not for me.<br /><br />There are some good scenes (an action sequence in the middle, and the conclusion) that kept me from giving this one star, but overall this is overblown and unwieldy. I get that this is a tribute to the ordinary folk who won the Battle of Britain, and as the grandchild of those people I like that part. But the 21st century historians who carry the story are passive, self-pitying, and all but intolerable. I also get that this story is about what the historians learn from courage and heroism of those around them ... but I really didn't want to spend nine hundred plus pages waiting for them to get their heads out of their asses.<br /><br />There is an interesting historical novel hidden in this book -- though I've read it before, and it's called <i>Mrs. Miniver</i>. There may even be a decent science fiction story in here, though I read that too when it was called "By His Bootstraps." But this book would have been better with about 500 pages of whining cut out of it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7539814487927017660" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/99747-chris?shelf=2014">2014</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1107443439" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3407213126674697853">No reviewette I write can possibly do this book (or really, the single book composed of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307.Blackout' title='Blackout by Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Blackout</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7519231.All_Clear' title='All Clear by Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>All Clear</a>) justice. Amazing. Epic. Subtlety of detail that had me paging back to confirm something hinted. Love. Loss. Loyalty. Uncertainty. Hope. Humor. Tragedy. The triumph of the human spirit. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis' title='Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Connie Willis</a> has outdone herself with this amazing tale of time travel so deeply rooted in place. Her Hugo and Nebula awards for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307.Blackout' title='Blackout by Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Blackout</a>/<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7519231.All_Clear' title='All Clear by Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>All Clear</a> bring her lifetime totals to eleven Hugos and seven Ne</span>
  <span id="freeText3407213126674697853" style="display:none">No reviewette I write can possibly do this book (or really, the single book composed of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307.Blackout" title="Blackout by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7519231.All_Clear" title="All Clear by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">All Clear</a>) justice. Amazing. Epic. Subtlety of detail that had me paging back to confirm something hinted. Love. Loss. Loyalty. Uncertainty. Hope. Humor. Tragedy. The triumph of the human spirit. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" title="Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Connie Willis</a> has outdone herself with this amazing tale of time travel so deeply rooted in place. Her Hugo and Nebula awards for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307.Blackout" title="Blackout by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a>/<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7519231.All_Clear" title="All Clear by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">All Clear</a> bring her lifetime totals to eleven Hugos and seven Nebulas. Make sure you have both books on hand and clear your schedule for a few days...</span>

<div id="review_2761509974" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2761509974" />
    <a title="YouKneeK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek"><img alt="YouKneeK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386369592p2/25923230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2761509974?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=completed-series">completed-series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2761509974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6351153006288614053"><i>All Clear</i> is the last book in the <i>Oxford Time Travel</i> series and the conclusion to the story begun in the previous book, <i>Blackout</i>. The story is set mostly during World War II and it picks up directly after the previous book.<br/><br/>It was mostly fast-paced and I really enjoyed it, although I did have issues with part of the premise of this series and how it was used to explain events in these last two books. I had a similar issue with the second book, but not the first one. The author takes a unique appr</span>
  <span id="freeText6351153006288614053" style="display:none"><i>All Clear</i> is the last book in the <i>Oxford Time Travel</i> series and the conclusion to the story begun in the previous book, <i>Blackout</i>. The story is set mostly during World War II and it picks up directly after the previous book.<br /><br />It was mostly fast-paced and I really enjoyed it, although I did have issues with part of the premise of this series and how it was used to explain events in these last two books. I had a similar issue with the second book, but not the first one. The author takes a unique approach toward explaining time travel, whether or not a time traveler might change the past, and what the consequences might be. At least, it was unique in my reading experience. However, I thought her approach was at least as problematic as those used by other authors if not more so. On the other hand, like I’ve said before, if you’re hoping for a completely logical story then you should probably not choose a time travel story. :)<br /><br />Aside from that complaint which I mostly just tried to ignore, this was a fun and interesting read. As with the previous book, I could really feel the emotions and fears that people would have experienced during the events described. It has quite a few twists and turns and the chapters alternate between different time periods which kept my mind engaged with trying to keep everything straight and to guess where things were going. I was able to guess a lot of it, but definitely not all of it.<br /><br />I have a few more comments for the spoiler tags:<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I really couldn’t buy into the concept of the net manipulating events by sending historians to exactly the right point where their personalities and decision-making processes would lead them to take actions that would somehow make everything come out all right in the end. Especially when those actions take place over months or years, and create a cascading effect with contemps who have had no interaction with the net whatsoever. There’s no way the net could manipulate these things without being sentient and omniscient. The net was invented to facilitate time travel, yet nobody seems to understand how it really works and they credit it with godlike abilities. I tried to ignore that aspect of the story and just read things as a series of unlikely but happy coincidences that happened to turn out mostly for the best. I was sad that Colin wasn’t able to save Mike though, after all Mike’s efforts over the years. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="6351153006288614053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2xdjVjCoMLlvtMTFIClDgI+d55UE94zRstT9vy2VwrpMI4Dn53mJY8RFzN0HLRR5+oNrOKUy6iI56Yq/sN/CNA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('NmES3hsvuiayMR5xEldjmdSgQPqOq6RRR1E1As+br0ChVfFvzP4D/BnAFmk1UzRgob7MVy9uwqLMbEICUtGvzg==')})
  }
//]]>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14905765354196211229">Constance Elaine Trimmer Willis is an American science fiction writer. She is one of the most honored science fiction writers of the 1980s and 1990s.<br/><br/>She has won, among other awards, ten Hugo Awards and six Nebula Awards. Willis most recently won a Hugo Award for All Seated on the Ground (August 2008). She was the 2011 recipient of the Damon Knight Memorial Grand Master Award from the Science Ficti</span>
  <span id="freeText14905765354196211229" style="display:none">Constance Elaine Trimmer Willis is an American science fiction writer. She is one of the most honored science fiction writers of the 1980s and 1990s.<br /><br />She has won, among other awards, ten Hugo Awards and six Nebula Awards. Willis most recently won a Hugo Award for All Seated on the Ground (August 2008). She was the 2011 recipient of the Damon Knight Memorial Grand Master Award from the Science Fiction Writers of America (SFWA).<br /><br />She lives in Greeley, Colorado with her husband Courtney Willis, a professor of physics at the University of Northern Colorado. She also has one daughter, Cordelia.<br /><br />Willis is known for her accessible prose and likable characters. She has written several pieces involving time travel by history students and faculty of the future University of Oxford. These pieces include her Hugo Award-winning novels <i>Doomsday Book</i> and <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> and the short story "Fire Watch," found in the short story collection of the same name.<br /><br />Willis tends to the comedy of manners style of writing. Her protagonists are typically beset by single-minded people pursuing illogical agendas, such as attempting to organize a bell-ringing session in the middle of a deadly epidemic (<i>Doomsday Book</i>), or frustrating efforts to analyze near-death experiences by putting words in the mouths of interviewees (<i>Passage</i>).</span>
  <a data-text-id="14905765354196211229" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
